<div id="review_450576546" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/450576546" />
    <a title="Stela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2585926-stela"><img alt="Stela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417111590p2/2585926.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/450576546?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stela" href="/user/show/2585926-stela">Stela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13409943-invitation-to-a-beheading">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">all my friends</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2585926-stela?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2585926-stela?shelf=magic-realism">magic-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2585926-stela?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2585926-stela?shelf=novel-of-ideas">novel-of-ideas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer450576546" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11771883786618189287"><b>
  <blockquote>The Light at the End of the Cave</blockquote>
</b><br/><br/>I can understand why Nabokov was accused of plagiarism when <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> was first published. At a first view and a very shallow first reading (or, let’s not be mean and say at a first level reading) it is indeed weirdly similar to <i>The Trial</i>, either in the plot construction, the main character attitude and the theme.<br/><br/>However there are so many major differences that save the book from being somehow a sequel of Kafka’s novel and put it on the general</span>
  <span id="freeText11771883786618189287" style="display:none"><b>
  <blockquote>The Light at the End of the Cave</blockquote>
</b><br /><br />I can understand why Nabokov was accused of plagiarism when <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> was first published. At a first view and a very shallow first reading (or, let’s not be mean and say at a first level reading) it is indeed weirdly similar to <i>The Trial</i>, either in the plot construction, the main character attitude and the theme.<br /><br />However there are so many major differences that save the book from being somehow a sequel of Kafka’s novel and put it on the general shelf of masterpieces with common themes – alienation and absurd of the existence that the author’s statement, that he was not familiar with Kafka’s works at the time, rings definitely true. <br /><br />On one hand, whereas K.’s drama is generated by an accusation he is informed of but never told what it was about, Cincinnatus knows very well the crime he was accused of: “the gnostic turpitude” of being opaque in a transparent world, real in a dream, solid among shades – in a word, different: <br /> <br /><blockquote>I am here through an error – not in this prison specifically – but in this whole, terrible, striped world; a world which seems not a bad example of amateur craftsmanship, but is in reality calamity, horror, madness, error – and look, the curio slays the tourist, the gigantic carved bear brings its wooden mallet down upon me.</blockquote><br /><br />Therefore, while K. is singularized by his unknown crime, Cincinnatus is unique from the beginning; a stranger among strangers and this is why he will stand tall when the world crumbles around him whereas K. dies “like a dog”, killed by his own kin.<br /><br />On the other hand, while <i>The Trial</i> satirizes bureaucracy and was seen as a grim premonition of the Holocaust, <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> even though can be read as a satire against totalitarianism is more than that: it is a reinterpretation of the myth of the cave. Like a Plato’s hero on his way towards knowledge, Cincinnatus grows tired of watching the shadows on the walls, and longs to discover who and what generated them:<br /><br /><blockquote>It exists, my dream world, it must exist since sure there must be an original of the clumsy copy.</blockquote><br /><br />The book describes mainly the struggle within the hero (by using the motive of the Romantic double), between the survival instinct that pushes him to act normal, to integrate, to mimic the others in order to forever remain chained in the apparent world and the call for knowledge that lures him out of the cage:<br /><br /><blockquote>Involuntarily yielding to the temptation of logical development, involuntarily (be careful, Cincinnatus!) forging into a chain all the things that were quite harmless as long as they remained unlinked, he inspired the meaningless with meaning and the lifeless with life.</blockquote><br /><br />A world where even “Socrates must decrease”, maybe unreal but not less oppressive, since everyone is a dummy, since the whole life is painted on a cardboard, a world where the appearance is taken for essence and the shadows claim to be the solid reality denying altogether any universe outside the cave. A world of imitations, whose theatrical falsity is depicted in that specific, unmistakable Russian way, with big gestures and highfalutin words and pitiful meaningless, a world that collapses into itself the very minute the hero leaves it, in a final image strangely distressing and triumphant at the same time:<br /><br /><blockquote>Everything was coming apart. Everything was falling. A spinning wind was picking up and whirling: dust, rags, chips of painted wood, bits of gilded plaster, pasteboard bricks, posters; an arid gloom fleeted; and amidst the dust, and the falling things, and the flapping scenery, Cincinnatus made his way in that direction where, to judge by the voices, stood beings akin to him.</blockquote> <br /><br />Is literature considered, Plato-style, a second-hand reality, an imitation of imitation? Is Cincinnatus the artist who struggles to give life to his work only to be imprisoned and almost destructed by it? Or, on the contrary, is art the only reality and life a mere imitation the hero frees himself of? <br /><br />So many challenging questions, but at the end of the day who cares, for an answer, anyway? As long as masterpieces like this one will continue to emerge…</span>
  <a data-text-id="11771883786618189287" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1423051033" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1423051033" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12933866-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444136022p2/12933866.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1423051033?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/12933866-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19017580-invitation-to-a-beheading">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1423051033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9700472956391293522">I find it difficult to believe Nabokov when in the preface to <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> he insists that he had no knowledge of Kafka when he wrote this book. This novel echoes <i>The Trial</i> in its plot and themes, not to mention the similarity in the protagonists names. Even the opening sentence appears to be a kind of homage; compare:<br/><br/><blockquote><i>"Someone must have been spreading slander about Josef K., for one morning he was arrested, though he had done nothing wrong."</i> <br/>- Kafka, <i>The Trial</i>.</blockquote><br/><br/><blockquote><i>"In accordance with th</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText9700472956391293522" style="display:none">I find it difficult to believe Nabokov when in the preface to <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> he insists that he had no knowledge of Kafka when he wrote this book. This novel echoes <i>The Trial</i> in its plot and themes, not to mention the similarity in the protagonists names. Even the opening sentence appears to be a kind of homage; compare:<br /><br /><blockquote><i>"Someone must have been spreading slander about Josef K., for one morning he was arrested, though he had done nothing wrong."</i> <br />- Kafka, <i>The Trial</i>.</blockquote><br /><br /><blockquote><i>"In accordance with the law the death sentence was announced to Cincinnatus C. in a whisper."</i><br />- Nabokov, <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i></blockquote><br /><br />Come on, Vladimir, who are you trying to fool? But though these similarities do invite comparison, I can understand his frustration at the facile, reductionist use of the “Kafkaesque” label, as simply equating the two novels would be a mistake: <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> is a very different novel to <i>The Trial</i>. It is a better novel, in my opinion (though I am a noted detractor of Kafka’s). To begin with, Nabokov’s prose is much more enjoyable to read, and the surreal and absurd elements are more imaginative, and more entertaining. Above all, <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> continues to develop its ideas as it progresses, rather than simply locking itself and the reader in a cycle of repeated frustration for one hundred and fifty pages. Nabokov’s novel also deals heavily with frustration, but his meaning and implication is more broad than Kafka’s. <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> is not about the restrictions imposed by the state, but those which we internalise and accept within our own lives. We are all in fact sentenced to live this absurd life, awaiting our own execution, and it is up to us to recognise our jailers. <br /> <br />I’m gradually coming back around to Nabokov, after the lingering disappointment of <i>Bend Sinister</i>, which I read almost two years ago. But in terms of quality, <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> is certainly closer to <i>Pnin</i> and <i>Pale Fire</i>, than it is to that book. One of his best, I think.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9700472956391293522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1271588187" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1271588187" />
    <a title="David Lentz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz"><img alt="David Lentz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473090881p2/3604115.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1271588187?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Lentz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Lentz" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz">David Lentz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780307787354" href="/book/show/10498516-invitation-to-a-beheading">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1271588187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer672422078286658151">It would seem that Nabokov entertains the idea that we live under a death sentence, which may be carried out at anytime with or without just cause by forces greater than we are as individuals with our limited scope of power and influence. The book is absurd, of course, in the true sense of the word insofar as it portrays life as essentially beyond our understanding except within the limited sensory confines of everyday life. It is a PoMo classic in the treatment of its themes and Nabokov transpo</span>
  <span id="freeText672422078286658151" style="display:none">It would seem that Nabokov entertains the idea that we live under a death sentence, which may be carried out at anytime with or without just cause by forces greater than we are as individuals with our limited scope of power and influence. The book is absurd, of course, in the true sense of the word insofar as it portrays life as essentially beyond our understanding except within the limited sensory confines of everyday life. It is a PoMo classic in the treatment of its themes and Nabokov transports his readers into a dreamlike existence. Cincinnatus would be quite at home with Joseph K. from Kafka and the cast from Beckett. That is, he is a victim of his own inability to understand his life and is trapped in a world far too vast to comprehend with pure reason alone. Yet within the dream state a certain logic prevails in the discourse which lends an air of credibility to the tale: we believe that in some totalitarian state the fate of Cincinnatus is all too real and was in fact inspired by the megalomania of tyrants past in Nabokov's native Russia and elsewhere. He brings to light the shadows of all the Angst of a convict on death row with his unbelief about his circumstances, his regrets for past lapses and failures, false hope of rescue, fear of the final indignity and pain. Yet Cincinnatus ascends the scaffold determined to face his end "By myself" and his redemption seems to be the victory of his will in the final pages of the novel. The engaging style of Nabokov is well worth noting: he is vivid, intelligent, original and credible as a narrator. Like Dostoyevsky in "The Idiot" he stays out of the way of his protagonist insofar as we experience his fate through his eyes. But his fellow characters shape the story line substantially with their own dialogue and actions driven by credible, all-too-human motives. At times, Cincinnatus is simply a silent witness, a felon consumed by dread after his conviction of "agnostic turpitude" of which he barely denies and as an Everyman must certainly be guilty even insofar as it will be the end of him. Nabokov peppers his narrative with nuance and his vision focuses upon a sensual reality which is both compelling and engaging: he really knows how to draw you into this dreamlike tale about the absurdity of life. By now, many of his themes from this novel may seem almost quaint within the PoMo catalogue but he was a force in defining a movement by virtue of the simplicity and power of his narrative gifts. My respect for Nabokov has gone up a notch or two after reading this brief book. Although some may not find satisfaction in the close of this tale, it is well worth reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="672422078286658151" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6971354485243183034">This was great, I love Nabokov when he`s not being so pompous in his prose.<br/><br/>But if I hear one more person label this as `Kafkaesque` I`ll smack them good!<br/><br/>Believe it or not, but generally I am not a fan of the absurd, but I loved the absurdity and helplessness in this novel. Imagine being condemned to death for an undefinable crime and not being told when it is that you will be executed(in Japan apparently pretty much no one knows when someone on death row dies until the actual day, yikes!) and ha</span>
  <span id="freeText6971354485243183034" style="display:none">This was great, I love Nabokov when he`s not being so pompous in his prose.<br /><br />But if I hear one more person label this as `Kafkaesque` I`ll smack them good!<br /><br />Believe it or not, but generally I am not a fan of the absurd, but I loved the absurdity and helplessness in this novel. Imagine being condemned to death for an undefinable crime and not being told when it is that you will be executed(in Japan apparently pretty much no one knows when someone on death row dies until the actual day, yikes!) and having to put up with quite some quirky characters in one of the most bizarre prisons I`ve ever experienced in reading, and I`ve read The Enormous Room by E.E Cummings!<br /><br />A few things were easy enough to predict, I won`t say so as to remove the possibility of potential spoilers, but it didn`t really detract from the reading experience. I must say that the ending was unexpected from an author like Nabokov, fans may be a little bit disappointed in that respect.<br /><br />Anyways, if you`re looking for something a little bizarre but still has literary merit, then this is certainly worth looking into. Others said it drags on here and there, but that`s just how Nabokov is, he grows on you like that.</span>

<div id="review_38903100" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38903100" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/38903100?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer38903100" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5309679389582892062">I see that the review on the GR home page for <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> compares it to Kafka. It's clear that Nabokov heard this rather more frequently than he wanted to, and was very tired of it. In the foreword to my edition, he has the following comment:<br/><br/>"Emigré reviewers, who were puzzled but liked it, thought they distinguished in it a "Kafkaesque" strain, not knowing that I had no German, was completely ignorant of modern German literature, and had not yet read any French or English transla</span>
  <span id="freeText5309679389582892062" style="display:none">I see that the review on the GR home page for <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> compares it to Kafka. It's clear that Nabokov heard this rather more frequently than he wanted to, and was very tired of it. In the foreword to my edition, he has the following comment:<br /><br />"Emigré reviewers, who were puzzled but liked it, thought they distinguished in it a "Kafkaesque" strain, not knowing that I had no German, was completely ignorant of modern German literature, and had not yet read any French or English translations of Kafka's works. No doubt, there do exist certain stylistic links between this book and, say, my earlier stories (or my later <i>Bend Sinister</i>); but there are none between it and <i>Le chateau</i> or <i>The Trial</i>."<br /><br />It's true that the word "Kafkaesque" is horribly overused. I love the postcoital scene in <i>Annie Hall</i> with Woody Allen and Shelley Duvall:<br /><br />				REPORTER<br /> 				<i>(Looking down at him)</i> <br />		I hope you don't mind that I took so long to finish.<br /><br />				ALVY<br /> 				<i>(Sighing)</i> <br />		Oh, no, no, don't be ... tsch ... don't be silly. You know, <br />			<i>(Yawning)</i> <br />		I'm startin' it-I'm startin' to get some feeling back in my jaw now.<br /><br />				REPORTER<br /> 			Oh, sex with you is really a Kafkaesque experience.<br /><br />				ALVY <br />		Oh, tsch, thank you. H'm.<br /><br />				REPORTER<br /> 			I mean that as a compliment.<br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1421685250" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12757134535716977687">I feel like I'm cheating Nabokov when I say I've read this book because with a book like the one here, it is an unending experience. One doesn't simply read and move past it but instead is invited by the text to re-read again and again, each time displaying a different layer, which like an onion's, is peeled off by each reading to reveal newer ones still. <br/><br/>Nabokov here plays jump rope with modernist and post-modernist tendencies. at one moment he is sad, at the other mad. While in places he wants</span>
  <span id="freeText12757134535716977687" style="display:none">I feel like I'm cheating Nabokov when I say I've read this book because with a book like the one here, it is an unending experience. One doesn't simply read and move past it but instead is invited by the text to re-read again and again, each time displaying a different layer, which like an onion's, is peeled off by each reading to reveal newer ones still. <br /><br />Nabokov here plays jump rope with modernist and post-modernist tendencies. at one moment he is sad, at the other mad. While in places he wants to revisit a glorious past, at others he celebrates a complete sense of spatio-temporal linearities. Combined with this masterful command of stylistics is the author's dazzling prose which is, fittingly, as lucid at times as it is near undecipherable at others. <br /><br />Such novels are not mere publications; they are achievements. I'm more glad than can be that this was my first Nabokov and not Lolita, which is the usual entry point into his oeuvre for most others.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12757134535716977687" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_10043616" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10043616" />
    <a title="Allie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/64642-allie"><img alt="Allie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199129436p2/64642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10043616?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allie" href="/user/show/64642-allie">Allie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Gen-X-ers, postmoderns</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64642-allie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/64642-allie?shelf=melancholy">melancholy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10043616" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14952799429462278200">I saw this book as a story about relationships. Cincinnatus is a prisoner for an absurd crime of personality, and his executioner cares for him and dotes on him, completely ignorant of any reason why the spitful Cincinnatus should dislike him. It teaches us about ourselves, and about the blurring of lines in our love relationships. <br/><br/>Sometimes, those who love us most, are the ones that imprison us or act as our executioners. Yet they love us, nonetheless. We think that those who love us will never</span>
  <span id="freeText14952799429462278200" style="display:none">I saw this book as a story about relationships. Cincinnatus is a prisoner for an absurd crime of personality, and his executioner cares for him and dotes on him, completely ignorant of any reason why the spitful Cincinnatus should dislike him. It teaches us about ourselves, and about the blurring of lines in our love relationships. <br /><br />Sometimes, those who love us most, are the ones that imprison us or act as our executioners. Yet they love us, nonetheless. We think that those who love us will never harm us and those who hate us always will, when actually the reality we experience is that the characters and the behaviors swap fairly often. We cannot hate those who love us simply because they have imprisoned us in some way, nor should we reject the kindness of someone who hates us, just on principle alone.<br /><br />In one famous scene, Cincinnatus is dancing with his jailer to some sort of waltz, and though he despises the idea of dancing with his executioner, he still feels sad when the dance has ended and he is returned to his cell. We are humans in the end, and the comforts and loves we feel are real even when they come from unlikely or unsavory places. We must come to terms with this and learn to enjoy them for what they are.<br /><br />I felt it was a great book, even though I'm not particularly into surrealism.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14952799429462278200" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1640221" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer928657137886184372">This was the first Nabokov novel I read, and I highly recommend it as an introduction. Although not as iconic as <i>Lolita</i> or as out-and-out brilliant as <i>Pale Fire</i>, this book both draws you in and keeps you at a distance, allowing you into its world but not inside the main character's head-- at least, not as much as his later works. Significantly, this is one of Nabokov's few novels (are there any other than this one?) that is not narrated in the first person. If prolonged, thoughtful imagery and t</span>
  <span id="freeText928657137886184372" style="display:none">This was the first Nabokov novel I read, and I highly recommend it as an introduction. Although not as iconic as <i>Lolita</i> or as out-and-out brilliant as <i>Pale Fire</i>, this book both draws you in and keeps you at a distance, allowing you into its world but not inside the main character's head-- at least, not as much as his later works. Significantly, this is one of Nabokov's few novels (are there any other than this one?) that is not narrated in the first person. If prolonged, thoughtful imagery and the occasional two-dollar word will hold your interest, then this book will give you one of the most fascinating endings in 20th-century literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="928657137886184372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_162970154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/162970154" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/162970154?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140100402" href="/book/show/936205.Invitation_to_a_Beheading">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=penguin-classics">penguin-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=borscht-and-kvass">borscht-and-kvass</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer162970154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13827442615323944926">It’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17877.The_House_of_the_Dead_' title='The House of the Dead  by Fyodor Dostoyevsky' rel='nofollow noopener'>The House of the Dead</a> meets Monty Python’s blacker moments. Nabokov wrote this in a fortnight, and although wired to his usual stylistic and linguistic arrogance, the story meanders in the way an undisciplined half-dream half-real semi-surrealist novel might. It's not quite Dostoevsky, not quite Gogol either.<br/><br/>I also began to mix up Cincinnatus with Ignatius J. Reilly from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24922.A_Confederacy_of_Dunces__Essential_penguin_' title='A Confederacy of Dunces (Essential.penguin) by John Kennedy Toole' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Confederacy of Dunces</a>, which wasn’t wholly random, as the novels aren’t too far off in terms of their dark humour. This</span>
  <span id="freeText13827442615323944926" style="display:none">It’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17877.The_House_of_the_Dead_" title="The House of the Dead  by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">The House of the Dead</a> meets Monty Python’s blacker moments. Nabokov wrote this in a fortnight, and although wired to his usual stylistic and linguistic arrogance, the story meanders in the way an undisciplined half-dream half-real semi-surrealist novel might. It's not quite Dostoevsky, not quite Gogol either.<br /><br />I also began to mix up Cincinnatus with Ignatius J. Reilly from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24922.A_Confederacy_of_Dunces__Essential_penguin_" title="A Confederacy of Dunces (Essential.penguin) by John Kennedy Toole" rel="nofollow noopener">A Confederacy of Dunces</a>, which wasn’t wholly random, as the novels aren’t too far off in terms of their dark humour. This novel withers under the punitive glee Nabokov takes in human suffering and becomes a swamp of language, fantasy and metaphor in a way that utterly supplants the story. Nice title, though.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13827442615323944926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1648346065" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1648346065" />
    <a title="Bryn Hammond" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7224461-bryn-hammond"><img alt="Bryn Hammond" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523356367p2/7224461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1648346065?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryn Hammond" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryn Hammond" href="/user/show/7224461-bryn-hammond">Bryn Hammond</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7224461-bryn-hammond?shelf=novelists-or-shorts">novelists-or-shorts</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1648346065" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15352215852303472100">Sizzling prose, often.<br/><br/>I think chapter 8 is going to become one of my personal classics: soliloquy of a condemned prisoner. This, with his other passages in solitary and the ending, make a worthy entry in anti-death penalty fiction, alongside such Russians as Leonid Andreyev (<i>Seven Who Were Hanged</i>) and Dostoyevsky (<i>The Idiot</i>). Nabokov’s dad and granddad both worked against the death penalty in government in Russia.<br/><br/>The bizarre farce… I only reconciled to after being guided to look at the book as a</span>
  <span id="freeText15352215852303472100" style="display:none">Sizzling prose, often.<br /><br />I think chapter 8 is going to become one of my personal classics: soliloquy of a condemned prisoner. This, with his other passages in solitary and the ending, make a worthy entry in anti-death penalty fiction, alongside such Russians as Leonid Andreyev (<i>Seven Who Were Hanged</i>) and Dostoyevsky (<i>The Idiot</i>). Nabokov’s dad and granddad both worked against the death penalty in government in Russia.<br /><br />The bizarre farce… I only reconciled to after being guided to look at the book as a quite specific satire on Soviet official philosophy of life, and its intellectual background in 19thC radical circles – the materialist, scientific-determinist school of Chernyshevsky, and Lenin after him, and that lot. Seen in this focus, I get it. Example: I put the novel on pause and under suspicion when I met the wife (I guess I had trust issues left over from <i>Lolita</i>); until I figured out she’s a satire of open marriages as advocated in Chernyshevsky’s novel, which Dostoyevsky satirised too in <i>Demons</i>. Indeed Dostoyevsky (my favourite author if you didn’t know) spent his latter life in struggle against this materialist-determinist tide in radical thought, so I’m up for novel by Nabokov against it. <br /><br />People have no personhood, except for Cincinnatus, who has 'gnostical turpitude' (his capital crime) because he has a subjectivity which science and other people's eyes cannot plumb. He has glimmerings of a day when the shoddy farce/philosophy of life around him blows away and people are allowed to be real again. In fact he looks forward to the 21st century for a life 'ennobled, spiritualised'... I don't know whether we can help him. <br /><br />I tend to be impatient of writers-writing-about-writing or similar circuitous topics; given Nabokov's disengaged stance, this is often taken for one. I'll go with the above interpretation, which makes sense to me. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15352215852303472100" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1088904097" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1088904097" />
    <a title="Ioana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1721356-ioana"><img alt="Ioana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505655996p2/1721356.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1088904097?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ioana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ioana" href="/user/show/1721356-ioana">Ioana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=dystopic-fiction">dystopic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=favorite-headtrips">favorite-headtrips</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=phenomenology">phenomenology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=russian-lit">russian-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1088904097" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8120339605656944949">100 stars! This is by far one of the most absurd, imaginative, and metaphorically insightful works of art I have ever encountered - it is what I would imagine a Dali painting to be if it were a novel. It is also brilliantly written.<br/><br/>Invitation to a Beheading is quite phenomenological in tone (in the tradition of Husserl, but more resembling Gaston Bachelard's phenomenology), serving to snap us out of our familiarity and out of our forgetting of the nature of our reality by continually inserting t</span>
  <span id="freeText8120339605656944949" style="display:none">100 stars! This is by far one of the most absurd, imaginative, and metaphorically insightful works of art I have ever encountered - it is what I would imagine a Dali painting to be if it were a novel. It is also brilliantly written.<br /><br />Invitation to a Beheading is quite phenomenological in tone (in the tradition of Husserl, but more resembling Gaston Bachelard's phenomenology), serving to snap us out of our familiarity and out of our forgetting of the nature of our reality by continually inserting the ridiculous into the narrative: a family who brings their furniture with them to jail for a brief visit of an inmate; a spider who inhabits a cell with the protagonist and who is fed and coddled; an execution ceremony which resembles a circus/variety-show act; chairs and furniture that move during the night, "and never spend the night in the same spot twice"; and seemingly nonsensical meanderings such as the observation that "an insane man mistakes his visiting kin for galaxies, logarithms, low-haunched hyenas". <br /><br />I wouldn't say I *enjoyed* this work - though the book was relatively short, it took me weeks to trudge through it; still, it quickly became one of my "favorites", and a work I would recommend to any of my literary friends above others in a heartbeat. There is no question that, although, as some have complained, this work lacks a "plot" or "character development", it is nevertheless a surreal masterpiece that reveals the absurdity in our own (moral, social) conventions.<br /><br />HIGHLY recommended, though you probably will not enjoy it if you are looking for a book with a "plot" and if you are easily frustrated by one in which not much seems to "happen". Invitation to a Beheading is more like philosophy than it is a novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8120339605656944949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_176873678" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176873678" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176873678?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=novel">novel</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176873678" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15849154221919224806">One of the novels that Nabokov wrote while living in Berlin. Unlike <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8151.Laughter_in_the_Dark' title='Laughter in the Dark by Vladimir Nabokov' rel='nofollow noopener'>Laughter in the Dark</a> it has a fantastical setting. The story is very simple - a man sits in his cell waiting to be executed, the action takes place over a period of days, or possibly weeks at the most. I remember the impression it made on me as I finished the book, feeling sick with my legs trembling.<br/><br/>As with <i>Laughter in the Dark</i> it strikes me as a fear based novel, Nabokov would probably dislike my slipping my hands like a phren</span>
  <span id="freeText15849154221919224806" style="display:none">One of the novels that Nabokov wrote while living in Berlin. Unlike <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8151.Laughter_in_the_Dark" title="Laughter in the Dark by Vladimir Nabokov" rel="nofollow noopener">Laughter in the Dark</a> it has a fantastical setting. The story is very simple - a man sits in his cell waiting to be executed, the action takes place over a period of days, or possibly weeks at the most. I remember the impression it made on me as I finished the book, feeling sick with my legs trembling.<br /><br />As with <i>Laughter in the Dark</i> it strikes me as a fear based novel, Nabokov would probably dislike my slipping my hands like a phrenologist over the bumps in his skull, but my impression would be that for both novels the emotion came first, here the horror of awaiting execution, the story is stripped away so there is only that fact and the reader's emotional response to it.<br /><br />I thought I might return to the book, I had borrowed it from the town library, along with many Graham Green novels, but in the intervening years some cultural shift has taken place and both authors have disappeared not only from the town library, but according to the catalogue from virtually all the libraries in the county. Time moves on, tastes change.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15849154221919224806" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2022773079" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2022773079" />
    <a title="Marianna Neal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13007653-marianna-neal"><img alt="Marianna Neal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572593566p2/13007653.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2022773079?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marianna Neal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marianna Neal" href="/user/show/13007653-marianna-neal">Marianna Neal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141185606" href="/book/show/12186.Invitation_to_a_Beheading">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13007653-marianna-neal?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13007653-marianna-neal?shelf=read-in-russian">read-in-russian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13007653-marianna-neal?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13007653-marianna-neal?shelf=vladimir-nabokov">vladimir-nabokov</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2022773079" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15030542885932748453">A complex, allegorical, existentialist work of fiction. One reading is definitely not enough to fully grasp everything Nabokov was trying to convey here. I find it interesting that Nabokov himself said this is not a political piece, and that this takes place around the year 3000 in Russia, and yet thematically so much of the novel has to do with failure to conform, being different, being hard to predict and understand, being hard to see through—this is the crime of Cincinnatus C. for which he is</span>
  <span id="freeText15030542885932748453" style="display:none">A complex, allegorical, existentialist work of fiction. One reading is definitely not enough to fully grasp everything Nabokov was trying to convey here. I find it interesting that Nabokov himself said this is not a political piece, and that this takes place around the year 3000 in Russia, and yet thematically so much of the novel has to do with failure to conform, being different, being hard to predict and understand, being hard to see through—this is the crime of Cincinnatus C. for which he is to be executed. The author takes a personal approach, examining the soul and the individual rather than focusing on any kind of totalitarian rule or harshness of law. In fact, everyone and everything around the main character has a touch of absurdism to it, with somewhat of a comedic touch. However, I don't believe the popular comparisons to Kafka are fair here—they only work on a very surface level, but once you dig deeper <i>Invitation to a Beheading</i> goes for a different focus and meaning. I'm going to revisit Kafka's <i>The Trial</i> though, just to make sure I'm not losing my mind here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15030542885932748453" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1047474404" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1047474404" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8311987-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411759189p2/8311987.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1047474404?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/8311987-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8311987-david?shelf=russian-literature">russian-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1047474404" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13693748514811094891">I would compare reading this book to analyzing a surrealist painting; in that there can be many possible explanations for what is going on in the painting (or novel), the motives behind the painting (or novel), and what there is to be learned, if indeed there is anything to be learned.<br/><br/>Cincinnatus, the protagonist, is convicted of a nebulous crime, for which the penalty is death, but at an unknown date. Is Cincinnatus dreaming? Has he hallucinated the entire affair? There is certainly an element </span>
  <span id="freeText13693748514811094891" style="display:none">I would compare reading this book to analyzing a surrealist painting; in that there can be many possible explanations for what is going on in the painting (or novel), the motives behind the painting (or novel), and what there is to be learned, if indeed there is anything to be learned.<br /><br />Cincinnatus, the protagonist, is convicted of a nebulous crime, for which the penalty is death, but at an unknown date. Is Cincinnatus dreaming? Has he hallucinated the entire affair? There is certainly an element of the absurd in the novel, and I must in some ways compare it to The Trial. But whereas the trial, and Kafka, focus on the absurdity of attempting to change your fate while stoically moving forward, Nabokov's novel reads more like a fever-dream.<br /><br />Time seems to pass in the novel, but not in conventional methods. Certainly, days pass, but the real locomotive of time, the date of his beheading, is alluded to by subtler means, the insects in his room being devoured by the spider, culminating in the beautiful moth, the length of Cincinnatus' pencil, and the amount of paper he has left to write upon. <br /><br />Time also passes nearer to his beheading by the increasing psychological tortures he endures; they become more acute as his end nears. It reminded me of the torture of Tantalus, where both food and drink seem to be within reach, but elude him when he reached for them. This happened to Cincinnatus when each time the hope of escape, hope, or epiphany dangled before him before being dashed into despair.<br /><br />What was Cincinnatus' crime? In my estimation, if the whole scenario were not a hallucination or dream, it would be being unable or unwilling to fit into society.<br /><br />The ending made me question whether the prison, the crime, the sentence, and even the characters were real. The fortress begins to collapse and turn into a labyrinth, Cincinnatus realizes the absurdity of his situation, refuses to accept it, and walk away as the world he was trapped in shrinks, fades away, and vanishes, as he travels towards voices he imagines to be like-minded with himself. Can an ending be more surreal than that? I don't know if I have read one at this point in my life. <br /><br />I'm sure there was much more nuance and imagery in this book I failed to pick up on or don't feel I have the time or energy to address, such as the absurdity of the executioner getting to know, and purportedly love, the one he is going to execute. Perhaps Nabokov was alluding to the fact that being the object of someone's affection can itself be a prison? I don't feel I have the authority to say. In any case, the book is very thought provoking.<br /><br />4/5</span>
  <a data-text-id="13693748514811094891" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_603560662" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/603560662" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4015835-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420385695p2/4015835.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/603560662?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4015835-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780307787354" href="/book/show/10498516-invitation-to-a-beheading">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=translated-fiction">translated-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=1930s">1930s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=classics-club">classics-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=eastern-european-literature">eastern-european-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer603560662" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14957170309944597914">In this bizarre and irrational world, Cincinnatus has been convicted and condemned to death by beheading for gnostical turpitude, an imaginary crime with no definition. Cincinnatus spends his remaining days in prison where he is visited by the chimerical jailers, an executioner who masquerades as a prisoner, and his in-laws. When Cincinnatus is finally brought out to be executed, he simply wills his executioners out of existence: they disappear, along with the whole world they inhabit.<br/><br/>There is n</span>
  <span id="freeText14957170309944597914" style="display:none">In this bizarre and irrational world, Cincinnatus has been convicted and condemned to death by beheading for gnostical turpitude, an imaginary crime with no definition. Cincinnatus spends his remaining days in prison where he is visited by the chimerical jailers, an executioner who masquerades as a prisoner, and his in-laws. When Cincinnatus is finally brought out to be executed, he simply wills his executioners out of existence: they disappear, along with the whole world they inhabit.<br /><br />There is no denying that Invitation to a Beheading is a weird novel; often compared with Franz Kafka’s The Castle, it is important to know that Vladimir Nabokov had not read any German novels, let alone Kafka when writing this. The reason this is important is to avoid trying to compare the two novels; sure they have similarities but they are still also vastly different. Originally published as a serial, with the title Sovremennye Zapiski (Contemporary Notes), Nabokov has stated while Lolita holds his greatest affection, this novel holds his greatest esteem.<br /><br />While people call this Kafkaesque, the impossible and dreamlike world reminds me more of Haruki Murakami’s style. From the very start the reader understands there is something not right about this world, this reminded me of Hard-Boiled Wonderland and the End of the World. I got the feeling that this wasn’t reality but a world constructed in Cincinnatus’ mind based on his fears, doubts, and insecurities. Cincinnatus’ enemy is the society he’s created and the people of that society act according to ridiculous rules that have been set. We never know what gnostical turpitude is and this will probably remind people of Kafka’s The Trial. Cincinnatus is rebelling against the construction of this reality and the rules the people of this society observe and perhaps this is what makes him a criminal.<br /><br />Maybe gnostical turpitude is the crime of being different from all the other people in this reality. Maybe Cincinnatus is being oppress for his ideas and his nature. Maybe he is so different from everyone around him; he has an internal depth that the others lack. A lot like Lolita, Vladimir Nabokov has a way about his writing that just leaves you with so many questions that you need to think through, this whole reality and society leaves me perplexed. Though this is the point; life isn’t simple and being an outsider sometimes feels like you are Cincinnatus in a bizarre reality.<br /><br />While this book primarily looks at society and oppression it also looks at human connection. Cincinnatus desires to connect with his wife Marthe, despite her unfaithfulness and lack of concern for him. The one thing he craves the most is to make a connection and she felt like the logical choice; also the fact that he loved her helped. He begs her to come alone and reveal her true self to him but there is always something that interferes with the communicating.<br /><br />While this was a very odd book, Vladimir Nabokov is just a brilliant writer and that really makes up for the weirdness. Also the weird and bizarre act as motifs within the narrative and without the symbolism and meaning it would just be trippy book. Nabokov does a good job of weaving his messages and ideas while entertaining the reader in unexpected ways. Most people only ever read Lolita by Vladimir Nabokov and I think that means they miss out on his brilliance, I hope to read more; currently on my To Be Read list is Mary, Pnin and Pale Fire. Are there any others I should add?<br /><br />This review originally appeared on my blog; <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://literary-exploration.com/2013/05/19/book-review-invitation-to-a-beheading/">http://literary-exploration.com/2013/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14957170309944597914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('p01egQOD0Uwta6ngvGzjhB924RgVOTFgh/uv9xLcGh5nGrUmQ8WbQMD+CWp0jiPbYJ4GKqgPhP4I3o5NfhXgDg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('+8cIxOhQf1MinksEVG21G8LVujoxC2L5ehbunMf/4CM7kONjqBY1X88L646cj3VEvT1dCIw912f1M88mqzYaMw==')})
  }

A cold, snowy morning.Andrei Danilovich Komiaga is fast asleep. A scream, a moan, and a death rattle slowly pull him out of his drunken stupor—but wait, that’s just his ring tone. And so begins another day in the life of an oprichnik, one of the czar’s most trusted courtiers—and one of the country’s most feared men.Welcome to the new New Russia, where futuristic technology and the draconian codes of Ivan the Terrible are in perfect synergy. Corporal punishment is back, as is a divine monarch, but these days everyone gets information from high-tech news bubbles, and the elite get high on hallucinogenic, genetically modified fish.Over the course of one day, Andrei Komiaga will bear witness to—and participate in—brutal executions; extravagant parties; meetings with ballerinas, soothsayers, and even the czarina. He will rape and pillage, and he will be moved to tears by the sweetly sung songs of his homeland. He will consume an arsenal of drugs and denounce threats to his great nation’s morals. And he will fall in love—perhaps even with a number of his colleagues.Vladimir Sorokin, the man described by Keith Gessen (in The New York Review of Books) as “[the] only real prose writer, and resident genius” of late-Soviet fiction, has imagined a near future both too disturbing to contemplate and too realistic to dismiss. But like all of his best work, Sorokin’s new novel explodes with invention and dark humor. A startling, relentless portrait of a troubled and troubling empire, Day of the Oprichnik is at once a richly imagined vision of the future and a razor-sharp diagnosis of a country in The novel\'s vision of Soviet life in the 1930s is so ferociously accurate that it could not be published during its author\'s lifetime and appeared only in a censored edition in the 1960s. Its truths are so enduring that its language has become part of the common Russian speech.\n\nOne hot spring, the devil arrives in Moscow, accompanied by a retinue that includes a beautiful naked witch and an immense talking black cat with a fondness for chess and vodka. The visitors quickly wreak havoc in a city that refuses to believe in either God or Satan. But they also bring peace to two unhappy Muscovites: one is the Master, a writer pilloried for daring to write a novel about Christ and Pontius Pilate; the other is Margarita, who loves the Master so deeply that she is willing literally to go to hell for him. What ensues is a novel of inexhaustible energy, humor, and philosophical depth, a work whose nuances emerge for the first time in Diana Burgin\'s and Katherine Tiernan O\'Connor\'s splendid English It is a set text in most New Zealand courses in universities, and is often grossly misrepresented as a kind of celebration of the Kiwi bloke going it alone, getting offside with the law and women, and making a fist of it on his own terms. It also has been glibly accused of misogyny and racism. For all its local emphases and colour, the novel must be read in the context of post-war Europe, as it takes a hard look at the reality of ‘ordinary’ life, without the self-congratulatory assurances common to both British and New Zealand conservatism. The starkness of the novel is also a philosophical one. Such values as emerge are what the individual manages to put together as the historical moment allows—fiction as existentialism, before such a term became modish. At the same time as he was working on the novel, Mulgan edited for Victor Gollancz Poems of Freedom, an anthology of poets who ‘were unafraid’, and whom W.H. <script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_8588158'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8588158-the-precipice?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Precipice<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/5326370.Ivan_Goncharov?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Ivan Goncharov<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.04 avg rating &mdash; 425 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1869\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer9648718758733784842\">Ivan Alexandrovich Goncharov (1812-1891) was one of the leading members of the great circle of Russian writers who, in the middle of the nineteenth century, gathered around the Sovremmenik (Contemporary) under Nekrasov\'s editorship-a circle including<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText9648718758733784842\" style=\"display:none\">Ivan Alexandrovich Goncharov (1812-1891) was one of the leading members of the great circle of Russian writers who, in the middle of the nineteenth century, gathered around the Sovremmenik (Contemporary) under Nekrasov\'s editorship-a circle including Turgenev, Dostoyevsky, Tolstoy, Byelinsky, and Herzen. He had not the marked genius of the first three of these; but that he is so much less known to the western reader is perhaps also due to the fact that there was nothing sensational either in his life or his literary method. Set in the twenty-sixth century AD, We is the classic dystopian novel and was the forerunner of works such as George Orwell\'s 1984 and Aldous Huxley\'s Brave New World. It was suppressed for many years in Russia and remains a resounding cry for individual freedom, yet is also a powerful, exciting and vivid work of science fiction.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5152.Vladimir_Nabokov">About Vladimir Nabokov</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5152.Vladimir_Nabokov"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1482502806p3/5152.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/5152.Vladimir_Nabokov">Vladimir Nabokov
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
10,811
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer69630743030430073">Russian: <b>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15904726._____________________________' title='Владимир Владимирович Набоков' rel='nofollow noopener'>Владимир Владимирович Набоков</a>
</b>.<br/><br/>Vladimir Vladimirovich Nabokov, also known by the pen name Vladimir Sirin, was a Russian-American novelist. Nabokov wrote his first nine novels in Russian, then rose to international prominence as a master English prose stylist. He also made significant contributions to lepidoptery, and had a big interest in chess problems.<br/><br/>Nabokov's <i>Lolita</i> (1955) is frequ</span>
  <span id="freeText69630743030430073" style="display:none">Russian: <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15904726._____________________________" title="Владимир Владимирович Набоков" rel="nofollow noopener">Владимир Владимирович Набоков</a>
</b>.<br /><br />Vladimir Vladimirovich Nabokov, also known by the pen name Vladimir Sirin, was a Russian-American novelist. Nabokov wrote his first nine novels in Russian, then rose to international prominence as a master English prose stylist. He also made significant contributions to lepidoptery, and had a big interest in chess problems.<br /><br />Nabokov's <i>Lolita</i> (1955) is frequently cited as his most important novel, and is at any rate his most widely known one, exhibiting the love of intricate wordplay and descriptive detail that characterized all his works.<br /><br />Lolita was ranked fourth in the list of the Modern Library 100 Best Novels; Pale Fire (1962) was ranked 53rd on the same list, and his memoir, Speak, Memory (1951), was listed eighth on the publisher's list of the 20th century's greatest nonfiction. He was also a finalist for the National Book Award for Fiction seven times. </span>
  <a data-text-id="69630743030430073" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.

last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
